[1mุนุงู [0m

Two books by Walter Laqueur, [3mTerrorism [0m(London: Little, Brown, 1977) and [3mThe Terrorism Reader [0m(London: Wildwood House, 1979) 
provide a concise historical background; his more recent [3mThe New Terrorism: Fanaticism and the Arms of Mass Destruction [0m(Oxford: 
Oxford University Press, 1999) is more prolix and more alarmist, with an extensive bibliographical essayโwhose only major lacuna 
is, oddly, the topic of โfanaticismโ, so loudly announced in the bookโs title but sketchily treated in the text. Grant Wardlaw, [3m
Political Terrorism: Theory, Tactics and Countermeasures [0m(Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1982, 1990) is a judicious 
analysis, while Bruce Hoffman, [3mInside Terrorism [0m(London: Victor Gollancz, 1998) is a useful survey from the Rand Corporation 
perspective, hard-nosed and unsentimental. For a more radical perspective, see Richard Falk, [3mRevolutionaries and Functionaries: 
The Dual Face of Terrorism [0m(New York: Dutton, 1988). Louise Richardson, [3mWhat Terrorists Want: Understanding the Terrorist Threat [0m
(London: John Murray, 2006), and Richard English, [3mTerrorism: How to Respond [0m(Oxford University Press, 2009) thoughtfully analyse 
historical experience. In the spate of books following 9/11, one or two are still worth reading, such as Strobe Talbott and Nayan 
Chanda (eds.), [3mThe Age of Terror [0m(New York: Basic Books, 2001). Cindy C. Combs, [3mTerrorism in the Twenty-First Century [0m(Upper 
Saddle River, NJ: Prentice Hall, 1997) is a college textbook that reveals much conventional wisdom on the subject. 

[1mุฅุฑูุงุจ ุงูุฏููุฉ [0m

Most writing on terror is workmanlike rather than brilliant, but an important exception is Eugene V. Walterโs essay in historical 
anthropology, [3mTerror and Resistance [0m(New York: Oxford University Press, 1969). Most conservative writers avoid the subject of 
state terror (the extensive section headed โstate terrorismโ in Laqueurโs [3mNew Terrorism [0m, for instance, proves to be all about 
โstate-sponsoredโ terrorโa completely different subjectโon the part of the USSR, Libya, Iran, and Iraq), so much of the commentary 
comes from a radical perspective: a fair example is William D. Perdue, [3mTerrorism and the State [0m(New York: Praeger, 1989). 
Alexander George (ed.), [3mWestern State Terrorism [0m(Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1991) contains several challenging essays, 
including a fierce critique of โThe Discipline of Terrorologyโ by the editor, who notes that his prime subject, Paul Wilkinson, 
โunlike many in this area, is not a raving madmanโ. There is a comparative study of two Latin American cases in David Pion-Berlin, [3m
The Ideology of State Terror: Economic Doctrine and Political Repression in Argentina and Peru [0m(Boulder, CO: L. Rienner, 1989). 

[1mุงูุฅุฑูุงุจ ุงูุซูุฑู [0m

The classic study of Russian populism is Franco Venturi, [3mRoots of Revolution [0m(New York: Knopf, 1960). Zeev Ivianski, [3mIndividual 
Terror: Theory and Practice [0m(Tel Aviv: ha-Kibbutz ha-Meuchad, 1977) is a lucid analysis. The contribution of women to 
revolutionary violence in Tsarist Russia is evoked in Vera Broido, [3mApostles into Terrorists [0m(New York: Viking Press, 1977). For 
the anarchists in general, see George Woodcock, [3mAnarchism [0m(Harmondsworth: Penguin, 1962), and in particular Martin A. Miller, [3m
Kropotkin [0m(Chicago: Chicago University Press, 1976). Martha Crenshawโs study of the FLN in Algeria, [3mRevolutionary Terrorism [0m
(Stanford: Hoover Institute Press, 1978) is an exemplary fusion of particular analysis with a wide theoretical vision. More 
idiosyncratic, but interesting, is Richard E. Rubinstein, [3mAlchemists of Revolution: Terrorism in the Modern World [0m(New York: Basic 
Books, 1987). A densely written but rewarding analysis of small-group terrorists in Italy and Germany can be found in Donatella 
della Porta, [3mSocial Movements, Political Violence and the State [0m(Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1995). 

[1mุงูุฅุฑูุงุจ ุงููููู [0m

On the IRA, M. L. R. Smith, [3mFighting for Ireland? The Military Strategy of the Irish Republican Movement [0m(London: Routledge, 1995) 
presents a level-headed academic examination; Patrick Bishop and Eamon Mallie, [3mThe Provisional IRA [0m(London: Heineman, 1987) and 
Peter Taylor, [3mProvos: The IRA and Sinn Fein [0m(London: Bloomsbury, 1997) are excellent journalistsโ investigations. 

ETA is, unsurprisingly, less well covered in English, but see John Sullivan, [3mETA and Basque Nationalism [0m(London: Routledge, 1988) 
and Joseba Zulaika, [3mBasque Violence [0m(Reno, NV: University of Nevada, 1988). 

The logic and methods of Zionist groups are illuminated in Yehuda Bauer, [3mFrom Diplomacy to Resistance [0m(New York: Atheneum, 1973); 
on the Lehi, see Joseph Heller, [3mThe Stern Gang: Ideology, Politics and Terror 1940โ1949 [0m(London: Frank Cass, 1995); on the Irgun, 
Menachem Beginโs memoir [3mThe Revolt [0m(London: W. H. Allen, 1979) is obligatory reading, while there is an absorbing account of their 
most famous operation in Thurston Clarke, [3mBy Blood and Fire: The Attack on the King David Hotel [0m(New York: Putnam, 1981). Less 
gripping than Begin, but useful, is General George Grivas, [3mGuerrilla Warfare and EOKAโs Struggle [0m(London: Longmans, 1964). 

[1mุงูุฅุฑูุงุจ ุงูุฏููู [0m

There is a consistently worthwhile collection of essays in Mark Juergensmeyer (ed.), [3mViolence and the Sacred in the Modern World [0m
(London: Frank Cass, 1992), and a longer study of โreligious nationalismโ by Juergensmeyer, [3mThe New Cold War? [0m(Berkeley: 
University of California Press, 1993). Juergensmeyerโs recent [3mTerrorism in the Mind of God [0m(Berkeley: University of California 
Press, 2000) engages with violence in three religious traditions, though it leaves hanging the question whether God is actor or 
audience in this terror process. There is a wide overview in Madawi al-Rasheed and Marat Shterin (eds.), [3mDying for Faith: 
Religiously Motivated Violence in the Contemporary World [0m(London: Tauris, 2009). Jihadist thinking is lucidly addressed in Malise 
Ruthven, [3mA Fury for God: The Islamist Attack on America [0m(London: Granta Books, 2002) and Mary R. Habeck, [3mKnowing the Enemy: 
Jihadist Ideology and the War on Terror [0m(New Haven and London: Yale University Press, 2006). Fred Halliday offers a typically 
sharp essay on โterrorisms in historical perspectiveโ, in [3mNation and Religion in the Middle East [0m(London: Saqi Books, 2000). For a 
remarkable rethinking, see Faisal Devji, [3mThe Terrorist in Search of Humanity: Militant Islam and Global Politics [0m(London: Hurst, 
2008). Of five illuminating volumes on fundamentalism by Martin E. Marty and R. Scott Appleby, see especially [3mFundamentalisms 
Comprehended [0m(Chicago: Chicago University Press, 1995). Martin Kramer provides a forensic analysis of Hezbollah in [3mThe Moral Logic 
of Hizbullah [0m(Tel Aviv: Tel Aviv University, 1987), and Ehud Sprinzak of Gush Emunim in [3mBrother Against Brother [0m(New York: Free 
Press, 1999). There are several interesting attempts to analyse suicide attacks, for instance Diego Gambetta (ed.), [3mMaking Sense 
of Suicide Missions [0m(Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2005). There are contrasting interpretations in Robert Pape, [3mDying to Win: 
The Strategic Logic of Suicide Terror [0m(New York: Random House, 2005) and Mia Bloom, [3mDying to Kill: The Allure of Suicide Terrorism [0m
(New York: Columbia University Press, 2005). For the social networks, see Marc Sageman, [3mLeaderless Jihad [0m(Philadelphia: 
Pennsylvania University Press, 2008). 

[1mููุงูุญุฉ ุงูุฅุฑูุงุจ [0m

John B. Wolf, [3mAntiterrorist Initiatives [0m(New York: Plenum Press, 1989) sets out the menu; Alex P. Schmid and Ronald D. Crelinsten 
(eds.), [3mWestern Responses to Terrorism [0m(London: Frank Cass, 1993) collects a number of helpful essays on both regional and 
theoretical issues. Benjamin Netanyahu (ed.), [3mTerrorism: How the West Can Win [0m(London: Farrar, Straus, Giroux, 1986) is a famous 
right-wing call to arms, whose loaded assumptions are loudly signalled in its title. The call to abandon conventional restraints 
is amplified in Alan Dershowitz, [3mWhy Terrorism Works [0m(New Haven and London: Yale University Press, 2002). There is a forensic 
examination of American antiterrorist methods before 9/11 in John K. Cooley, [3mUnholy Wars: Afghanistan, America and International 
Terrorism [0m(London: Pluto Press, 1999), and a remarkable personal account in Richard A. Clarke, [3mAgainst All Enemies: Inside 
Americaโs War on Terror [0m(London: Simon & Schuster, 2004). Later โwar on terrorโ strategy is assessed in Seth Jones, [3mIn the 
Graveyard of Empires: Americaโs War in Afghanistan [0m(New York: Norton, 2009). Christopher Hewitt, [3mThe Effectiveness of 
Anti-Terrorist Policies [0m(Langham, MD: University Press of America, 1984) is a rare attempt to find ways of measuring effects. For 
a pioneering example of โcritical terrorism studiesโ, see Richard Jackson, [3mWriting the War on Terrorism: Language, Politics and 
Counter-Terrorism [0m(Manchester: Manchester University Press, 2005). 

On the issue of democracy, Paul Wilkinson, [3mTerrorism Versus Democracy: The Liberal State Response [0m(London: Routledge, 2011) offers 
a commonsensical overview, though producing little evidence that terrorism threatens democracy as such. See also David A. Charters 
(ed.), [3mThe Deadly Sin of Terrorism: Its Effect on Democracy and Civil Liberty in Six Countries [0m(Westport, Conn.: Greenwood Press, 
1994). On the media in particular, see Alex P. Schmid and Janny de Graaf, [3mViolence as Communication: Insurgent Terrorism and the 
Western News Media [0m(Beverly Hills: Sage, 1982).
